         </div>
        </div>
       </header>
       <div class="postArticle-content js-postField js-notesSource js-trackedPost" data-post-id="b7b20a38438f" data-source="post_page" data-collection-id="3a8144eabfe3" data-tracking-context="postPage">
        <section name="c7ce" class="section section--body section--first section--last">
         <div class="section-divider">
          <hr class="section-divider">
         </div>
         <div class="section-content">
          <div class="section-inner sectionLayout--insetColumn">
           <h1 name="a85c" id="a85c" class="graf graf--h3 graf--leading graf--title">Nightmarishly good scraping with Nightmare.js and async/await</h1>
           <p name="3226" id="3226" class="graf graf--p graf-after--h3">Website scraping is a fact of life if youre doing data-journalism, and the traditional wisdom has always been to use something like Pythons Beautiful Soup library to make and parse requests. Ladies and gentlemen, let me tell you??the traditional wisdom is wrong! Use Nightmare.js!</p>
           <p name="233a" id="233a" class="graf graf--p graf--startsWithDoubleQuote graf-after--p"><em class="markup--em markup--p-em">Why?</em>, you ask? There are ultimately two ways you can write code to scrape a webpage:</p>
           <ol class="postList">
            <li name="03e3" id="03e3" class="graf graf--li graf-after--p">Make a request against a parameterized URL, retrieve the HTML at that address, then parse that somehow. Extract useful data and links for the next request; decipher the URL structure to run it as a loop.</li>
            <li name="806c" id="806c" class="graf graf--li graf-after--li">Automate a web-browser to make requests and navigate through a site, extracting data from each page.</li>
           </ol>
           <p name="6822" id="6822" class="graf graf--p graf-after--li"><a href="http://www.nightmarejs.org/" data-href="http://www.nightmarejs.org/" class="markup--anchor markup--p-anchor" rel="noopener" target="_blank">Nightmare.js</a> is the latter approach, and even though it seems similar to the first, there is one significant advantage??by being an actual web-browser (Its based on Electron, the same Google Chrome-derived framework that powers the Atom text editor) that can submit forms&nbsp;, run JavaScript and save cookies, your scraper will bypass a lot of the annoying code that can trip it up. Not only that, it means websites that render mostly on the client-side are still scrape-ableif ever youve been thrown by needing to make an AJAX request to return a form in your scraping, <strong class="markup--strong markup--p-strong"><em class="markup--em markup--p-em">today is your day to be awesome!</em></strong></p>
           <figure name="750b" id="750b" class="graf graf--figure graf-after--p">
            <div class="aspectRatioPlaceholder is-locked" style="max-width: 320px; max-height: 180px;">
             <div class="aspectRatioPlaceholder-fill" style="padding-bottom: 56.3%;"></div>
             <div class="progressiveMedia js-progressiveMedia graf-image" data-image-id="1*9t4Peti3LgCQiJP9STtmjg.gif" data-width="320" data-height="180">
              <img src="https://cdn-images-1.medium.com/freeze/max/60/1*9t4Peti3LgCQiJP9STtmjg.gif?q=20" crossorigin="anonymous" class="progressiveMedia-thumbnail js-progressiveMedia-thumbnail">
              <canvas class="progressiveMedia-canvas js-progressiveMedia-canvas"></canvas>
              <img class="progressiveMedia-image js-progressiveMedia-image" data-src="https://cdn-images-1.medium.com/max/1600/1*9t4Peti3LgCQiJP9STtmjg.gif">
              <noscript class="js-progressiveMedia-inner">
               <img class="progressiveMedia-noscript js-progressiveMedia-inner" src="https://cdn-images-1.medium.com/max/1600/1*9t4Peti3LgCQiJP9STtmjg.gif">
              </noscript>
             </div>
            </div>
            <figcaption class="imageCaption">
             Gif via 
             <a href="http://giphy.com/gifs/KlJ4wKeb8VMqs" data-href="http://giphy.com/gifs/KlJ4wKeb8VMqs" class="markup--anchor markup--figure-anchor" rel="nofollow noopener" target="_blank">http://giphy.com/gifs/KlJ4wKeb8VMqs</a>. Hey, want to know what a totally 
             <strong class="markup--strong markup--figure-strong"><em class="markup--em markup--figure-em">not awesome</em></strong>
             <em class="markup--em markup--figure-em"> thing to do before your first coffee? Search </em>
             <a href="http://giphy.com/search/nightmare" data-href="http://giphy.com/search/nightmare" class="markup--anchor markup--figure-anchor" rel="noopener" target="_blank"><em class="markup--em markup--figure-em">nightmare</em></a>
             <em class="markup--em markup--figure-em"> on Giphy.&nbsp;?</em>
            </figcaption>
           </figure>
           <p name="b420" id="b420" class="graf graf--p graf--startsWithDoubleQuote graf-after--figure"><em class="markup--em markup--p-em">But Ændrew!</em>, you say.<em class="markup--em markup--p-em"> JavaScript is really annoying and all that asynchronous single-threaded stuff is really hard to keep track of while writing simple scrapers!</em></p>
           <p name="0891" id="0891" class="graf graf--p graf-after--p">And youre not wrong! It can be kind of annoying and frustrating! But today were using Node 7 with native <strong class="markup--strong markup--p-strong"><em class="markup--em markup--p-em">async/await</em></strong>, which dramatically simplifies writing code to manage a big series of requests! This stuff is so new you had to use <a href="https://babeljs.io/" data-href="https://babeljs.io/" class="markup--anchor markup--p-anchor" rel="noopener" target="_blank">Babel</a> to get it to work until recentlylets live dangerously and use the latest version of NodeJS to do awesome things!</p>
           <h4 name="ccd0" id="ccd0" class="graf graf--h4 graf-after--p">Install NodeJS&nbsp;7</h4>
           <p name="ff23" id="ff23" class="graf graf--p graf-after--h4">First we need to install Node 7.x.</p>
           <p name="b0c4" id="b0c4" class="graf graf--p graf-after--p">On <strong class="markup--strong markup--p-strong">OS X</strong>, I prefer <a href="https://github.com/tj/n" data-href="https://github.com/tj/n" class="markup--anchor markup--p-anchor" rel="noopener" target="_blank"><strong class="markup--strong markup--p-strong"><em class="markup--em markup--p-em">n</em></strong></a> for managing NodeJS versions. Install via Homebrew:</p>
           <pre name="21ec" id="21ec" class="graf graf--pre graf-after--p">$ brew install n</pre>
           <p name="8500" id="8500" class="graf graf--p graf-after--pre">Then install the <strong class="markup--strong markup--p-strong"><em class="markup--em markup--p-em">absolute most cutting-edge, latest-core version of NodeJS </em></strong>by simply typing:</p>
           <pre name="7a24" id="7a24" class="graf graf--pre graf-after--p">$ n latest</pre>
           <p name="e114" id="e114" class="graf graf--p graf-after--pre"><strong class="markup--strong markup--p-strong">? Youre done! ? Skip forward!*</strong></p>
           <p name="ddcb" id="ddcb" class="graf graf--p graf-after--p">*<em class="markup--em markup--p-em">Oh, you dont have </em><a href="http://brew.sh/" data-href="http://brew.sh/" class="markup--anchor markup--p-anchor" rel="noopener" target="_blank"><em class="markup--em markup--p-em">Homebrew</em></a><em class="markup--em markup--p-em">? Uh, youll need to install XCode and a bunch of other crap to get that to work, which you might just not have time for right now. Just go to the </em><a href="https://nodejs.org" data-href="https://nodejs.org" class="markup--anchor markup--p-anchor" rel="noopener" target="_blank"><em class="markup--em markup--p-em">NodeJS</em></a><em class="markup--em markup--p-em"> website and use the installer. Choose the version on the right??Im using </em><a href="https://nodejs.org/dist/v7.2.0/node-v7.2.0.pkg" data-href="https://nodejs.org/dist/v7.2.0/node-v7.2.0.pkg" class="markup--anchor markup--p-anchor" rel="noopener" target="_blank"><strong class="markup--strong markup--p-strong"><em class="markup--em markup--p-em">7.2.0</em></strong></a><em class="markup--em markup--p-em">, though its quite likely that wont be the version listed there for longer than the next fifteen minutes. You want Node 7+ at any rate.</em></p>
           <p name="eb3b" id="eb3b" class="graf graf--p graf-after--p">On <strong class="markup--strong markup--p-strong">Windows</strong>, I think <a href="https://github.com/marcelklehr/nodist" data-href="https://github.com/marcelklehr/nodist" class="markup--anchor markup--p-anchor" rel="noopener" target="_blank">Nodist</a> might be your best bet? Check it out. I havent used Windows in like a decade though, so you probably know better than me. Also, if this all works in Windows, just know Ill be <em class="markup--em markup--p-em">hella </em>impressed with myself (alas, if not, please leave a comment ?).</p>
           <h4 name="22f7" id="22f7" class="graf graf--h4 graf-after--p">Initialise a new&nbsp;package</h4>
           <p name="bcd0" id="bcd0" class="graf graf--p graf-after--h4">Okay, lets create a new directory and initialise a package.json file there. If youre new to NodeJS and npm, <strong class="markup--strong markup--p-strong">package.json</strong> is used to manage dependencies and download stuff. Its neato.</p>
           <pre name="cb1d" id="cb1d" class="graf graf--pre graf-after--p">$ mkdir scraping-project<br>$ cd scraping-project<br><strong class="markup--strong markup--pre-strong">$ npm init -y</strong></pre>
           <p name="80b1" id="80b1" class="graf graf--p graf-after--pre">You should now be in a new directory called <strong class="markup--strong markup--p-strong">scraping-project/</strong> with a blank <strong class="markup--strong markup--p-strong">package.json</strong> file. The bold bit above generated the latter, if youre curious.</p>
           <p name="a230" id="a230" class="graf graf--p graf-after--p">Lets install Nightmare and get ready to scrape! Well also install <strong class="markup--strong markup--p-strong">d3-dsv </strong>so we can easily write our data out to CSV at the end:</p>
           <pre name="2dc2" id="2dc2" class="graf graf--pre graf-after--p">$ npm install nightmare d3-dsv --save</pre>
           <p name="442a" id="442a" class="graf graf--p graf-after--pre">This will download the above to the <strong class="markup--strong markup--p-strong">node_modules/</strong> folder within the current directory. You can also just clone <a href="https://github.com/aendrew/nightmare-tutorial" data-href="https://github.com/aendrew/nightmare-tutorial" class="markup--anchor markup--p-anchor" rel="noopener" target="_blank">this repo</a> and run <strong class="markup--strong markup--p-strong">npm install</strong> to get the completed tutorial.</p>
           <h4 name="abc1" id="abc1" class="graf graf--h4 graf-after--p">Map your scraping&nbsp;process</h4>
           <p name="eb9f" id="eb9f" class="graf graf--p graf-after--h4">Its worth planning your scrapers workflow before you code anythingfive minutes of planning is worth an hours worth of code. Generally, a scraping process is broken down into several parts, but usually it starts with some sort of unique identifier. This might be a product code, a company code, a postal code or some other identifier that is granular enough to let your scraper pick out the bits you need while it runs.</p>
           <p name="6b8b" id="6b8b" class="graf graf--p graf-after--p">In our example, were going to input land registry title numbers taken from a CSV file into the UK Land Registry to get the corresponding address. Were then going to output that as a CSV for later analysis. We may have gotten these original identifiers from some other process??for instance, they may have been compiled from reading through reams of legal documents, or even from another scraper.</p>
           <p name="d2cb" id="d2cb" class="graf graf--p graf-after--p">First, download the data from here:</p>
           <p name="d8c4" id="d8c4" class="graf graf--p graf-after--p"><a href="https://gist.githubusercontent.com/aendrew/76f3fae770e647ae7b0ea865ba0c4418/raw/eb047d0b74cc539a7fa873b1594e28e1dc310f6f/tesco-title-numbers.csv" data-href="https://gist.githubusercontent.com/aendrew/76f3fae770e647ae7b0ea865ba0c4418/raw/eb047d0b74cc539a7fa873b1594e28e1dc310f6f/tesco-title-numbers.csv" class="markup--anchor markup--p-anchor" rel="nofollow noopener" target="_blank">https://gist.githubusercontent.com/aendrew/76f3fae770e647ae7b0ea865ba0c4418/raw/eb047d0b74cc539a7fa873b1594e28e1dc310f6f/tesco-title-numbers.csv</a></p>
           <p name="c482" id="c482" class="graf graf--p graf-after--p">Save it as <strong class="markup--strong markup--p-strong">tesco-title-numbers.csv </strong>in your project directory.</p>
           <p name="d1a0" id="d1a0" class="graf graf--p graf-after--p">Were ready to start coding! Create a new file in your text editor of choice??Imma call mine <strong class="markup--strong markup--p-strong">index.js</strong> because Im an free-spirited, fun-loving individual, but you can call yours whatever.</p>
           <p name="f22e" id="f22e" class="graf graf--p graf-after--p">In your new&nbsp;.js file, write the following:</p>
           <pre name="d550" id="d550" class="graf graf--pre graf-after--p">const { csvFormat } = require('d3-dsv');<br>const Nightmare = require('nightmare');<br>const { readFileSync, writeFileSync } = require('fs');</pre>
           <pre name="0de3" id="0de3" class="graf graf--pre graf-after--pre">const numbers = readFileSync('./tesco-title-numbers.csv', <br>  {encoding: 'utf8'}).trim().split('\n');<br>console.dir(numbers);</pre>
           <p name="1dee" id="1dee" class="graf graf--p graf-after--pre">This imports a few things, including <strong class="markup--strong markup--p-strong">csvFormat</strong> from <strong class="markup--strong markup--p-strong">d3-dsv</strong> (which well use later to output our results as CSV), <strong class="markup--strong markup--p-strong">Nightmare</strong> itself, and a few functions from the NodeJS filesystem (<strong class="markup--strong markup--p-strong">fs</strong>) package. We then use <strong class="markup--strong markup--p-strong">readFileSync()</strong> to load in our CSV file and then convert it to an array by splitting the resultant string by the newline (<strong class="markup--strong markup--p-strong">\n</strong>) character. Note if that was a real CSV file (i.e., with a header row and whathaveyou) we could have parsed it into an array using <strong class="markup--strong markup--p-strong">csvParse()</strong> from the <strong class="markup--strong markup--p-strong">d3-dsv</strong> package, but given how simple our input is, that would be overkill in this instance. Lastly, we output the resulting array using <strong class="markup--strong markup--p-strong">console.dir()</strong>.</p>
           <p name="4b33" id="4b33" class="graf graf--p graf-after--p">In your project directory, run:</p>
           <pre name="5ebf" id="5ebf" class="graf graf--pre graf-after--p">$ node index.js</pre>
           <p name="b45b" id="b45b" class="graf graf--p graf-after--pre">And youll see an array of property title numbers. Fantastic, were well on our way.</p>
           <h4 name="936f" id="936f" class="graf graf--h4 graf-after--p">The Nightmare Cometh!</h4>
           <p name="fb51" id="fb51" class="graf graf--p graf-after--h4">Next thing to do is start scraping using Nightmare. Were going to run a loop to plug each of the title numbers into the form located at:</p>
           <p name="fd37" id="fd37" class="graf graf--p graf-after--p"><a href="https://eservices.landregistry.gov.uk/wps/portal/Property_Search" data-href="https://eservices.landregistry.gov.uk/wps/portal/Property_Search" class="markup--anchor markup--p-anchor" rel="noopener" target="_blank"><strong class="markup--strong markup--p-strong">https://eservices.landregistry.gov.uk/wps/portal/Property_Search</strong></a></p>
           <p name="30a4" id="30a4" class="graf graf--p graf-after--p">This site is hilariously coded and has totally unparseable URLs. The second we navigate anywhere, that already-long URL turns into a mess that looks like:</p>
           <pre name="9c0f" id="9c0f" class="graf graf--pre graf-after--p"><a href="https://eservices.landregistry.gov.uk/www/wps/portal/!ut/p/b1/04_Sj9CPykssy0xPLMnMz0vMAfGjzOKNjSxMDA1NjDwsjM3MDTxN3dyNDUNMjQ1MjPWDU_P0C7IdFQG9k5Tz/" data-href="https://eservices.landregistry.gov.uk/www/wps/portal/!ut/p/b1/04_Sj9CPykssy0xPLMnMz0vMAfGjzOKNjSxMDA1NjDwsjM3MDTxN3dyNDUNMjQ1MjPWDU_P0C7IdFQG9k5Tz/" class="markup--anchor markup--pre-anchor" rel="nofollow noopener" target="_blank">https://eservices.landregistry.gov.uk/www/wps/portal/!ut/p/b1/04_Sj9CPykssy0xPLMnMz0vMAfGjzOKNjSxMDA1NjDwsjM3MDTxN3dyNDUNMjQ1MjPWDU_P0C7IdFQG9k5Tz/</a></pre>
           <p name="8375" id="8375" class="graf graf--p graf-after--pre">Thats not even a serious query, thats just the search form. Good luck figuring out how to automate a query using just the URL! Whys there an exclamation mark in the middle of that? What is the significance of that really long indecipherable string? Were using Nightmare.js, though, so who cares? It doesnt matter.</p>
           <p name="64b5" id="64b5" class="graf graf--p graf-after--p">Lets write a function that gets the address from the land title record and returns it as an object. This is going to get a bit weird if youve ever written a bit of JavaScript before and not done a lot with promises, but please bear with me.</p>
           <p name="6532" id="6532" class="graf graf--p graf-after--p">Were going to make an <strong class="markup--strong markup--p-strong">async </strong>function, which is why I made you jump through the hoops of installing Node 7. We can use these by supplying this flag when invoking Node with the following syntax:</p>
           <pre name="03ea" id="03ea" class="graf graf--pre graf-after--p">$ node <strong class="markup--strong markup--pre-strong">--harmony</strong> &lt;file&gt;</pre>
           <p name="7671" id="7671" class="graf graf--p graf-after--pre">Marking a function as <strong class="markup--strong markup--p-strong">async</strong> lets the Node interpreter know the function will always return a <a href="https://developer.mozilla.org/en/docs/Web/JavaScript/Reference/Global_Objects/Promise" data-href="https://developer.mozilla.org/en/docs/Web/JavaScript/Reference/Global_Objects/Promise" class="markup--anchor markup--p-anchor" rel="noopener" target="_blank"><strong class="markup--strong markup--p-strong">promise</strong></a>??that is, a value that may eventually be resolved. The cool bit about this is that we can <strong class="markup--strong markup--p-strong">await</strong> other promises, and write code that acts normally even while a bunch of asynchronous processes are firing. Ill write another post shortly going into more detail about this all and why its awesome, but suffice to say for the moment that you need to pay attention to this because Nightmare returns promises for <em class="markup--em markup--p-em">everything</em>.</p>
           <p name="05d1" id="05d1" class="graf graf--p graf-after--p">Remove the console.dir line and replace it with the following:</p>
           <pre name="a2a2" id="a2a2" class="graf graf--pre graf-after--p">const START = '<a href="https://eservices.landregistry.gov.uk/wps/portal/Property_Search%27" data-href="https://eservices.landregistry.gov.uk/wps/portal/Property_Search'" class="markup--anchor markup--pre-anchor" rel="nofollow noopener" target="_blank">https://eservices.landregistry.gov.uk/wps/portal/Property_Search'</a>;</pre>
           <pre name="d37c" id="d37c" class="graf graf--pre graf-after--pre">const getAddress = async id =&gt; {};</pre>
           <p name="6764" id="6764" class="graf graf--p graf-after--pre">Here we assign our starting point and our <strong class="markup--strong markup--p-strong">getAddress()</strong> asynchronous function. If new to ES6, the <strong class="markup--strong markup--p-strong"><em class="markup--em markup--p-em">=&gt;</em></strong> bit means that its a function that is lexically scoped to the current block (but that doesnt matter because we wont be using the <strong class="markup--strong markup--p-strong"><em class="markup--em markup--p-em">this</em></strong> keyword anywhere; I mainly use fat arrow here for the sake of brevity). Were going to fill out that function by writing code between the curly braces:</p>
           <pre name="fbb2" id="fbb2" class="graf graf--pre graf-after--p">const getAddress = async id =&gt; {<br>  console.log(`Now checking ${id}`);<br>  const nightmare = new Nightmare({ show: true });</pre>
           <pre name="922e" id="922e" class="graf graf--pre graf-after--pre">// Go to initial start page, navigate to Detail search<br>  try {<br>    await nightmare<br>      .goto(START)<br>      .wait('.bodylinkcopy:first-child')<br>      .click('.bodylinkcopy:first-child');<br>  } catch(e) {<br>    console.error(e);<br>  }</pre>
           <pre name="d4f2" id="d4f2" class="graf graf--pre graf-after--pre">}</pre>
           <p name="f204" id="f204" class="graf graf--p graf-after--pre">Weve added a line to log which identifier were on and initialised Nightmare using the <strong class="markup--strong markup--p-strong"><em class="markup--em markup--p-em">new</em></strong> keyword, to which we pass an options object. In this instance, weve set the <strong class="markup--strong markup--p-strong">show </strong>property to <strong class="markup--strong markup--p-strong"><em class="markup--em markup--p-em">true</em></strong> so we can monitor what the browser is doing while it works.</p>
           <p name="2d26" id="2d26" class="graf graf--p graf-after--p">We then do our first action, which is wrapped in a <em class="markup--em markup--p-em">try/catch</em> block. One cool thing about async/await is that you handle errors using try/catch blocks, which is a pleasant and straightforward way for doing things when your code fails for whatever reason (and, if youve never scraped before trust me, it will, because web-servers are the <em class="markup--em markup--p-em">worst</em>, and especially if theyre managed by small government bodies). In this instance, we just log the exception because its clear enough from that whats happened.</p>
           <p name="42ad" id="42ad" class="graf graf--p graf-after--p">Inside of our <strong class="markup--strong markup--p-strong">try</strong> block, we have Nightmare finally do some actual work. We use the <strong class="markup--strong markup--p-strong">await</strong> keyword to ensure the promise returned by Nightmare is resolved before continuing, then tell it to go to our starting point, wait for the first element with the&nbsp;<strong class="markup--strong markup--p-strong"><em class="markup--em markup--p-em">.bodylinkcopy</em></strong> class to appear, then click that element. That will take us to the detailed search page, which we need for entering the property title number.</p>
           <p name="36bb" id="36bb" class="graf graf--p graf-after--p">Next we need to enter our property title number value into the correct search field. Add the following after the last try/catch block, inside our <strong class="markup--strong markup--p-strong">getAddress()</strong> function:</p>
           <pre name="62b0" id="62b0" class="graf graf--pre graf-after--p">// Type the title number into the appropriate box; click submit<br>try {<br>  await nightmare<br>    .wait('input[name="titleNo"]')<br>    .type('input[name="titleNo"]', id)<br>    .click('input[value="Search »"]');<br>} catch(e) {<br>  console.error(e);<br>}</pre>
           <p name="8118" id="8118" class="graf graf--p graf-after--pre">Much like the last action, this awaits Nightmare to resolve the promise returned by the queue of actions weve give it. First we tell it to wait for the correct input button to render on the page. Then we tell it to <strong class="markup--strong markup--p-strong">type</strong> in our property title <strong class="markup--strong markup--p-strong"><em class="markup--em markup--p-em">id</em></strong> number, which is our sole function argument for <strong class="markup--strong markup--p-strong">getAddress()</strong>. We finally <strong class="markup--strong markup--p-strong">click</strong> on the submit button to initiate our search.</p>
           <p name="1e95" id="1e95" class="graf graf--p graf-after--p">Okay, heres where things get challenging. Were now at a results page, and need to extract the proper data from the page. Add this new try/catch block after the last one:</p>
           <pre name="e702" id="e702" class="graf graf--pre graf-after--p">try {<br>  const result = await nightmare<br>    .wait('.w80p')<br>    .evaluate(() =&gt; {<br>      return [...document.querySelectorAll('.w80p')]<br>        .map(el =&gt; el.innerText);<br>    })<br>    .end();</pre>
           <pre name="4d7b" id="4d7b" class="graf graf--pre graf-after--pre">  return { id, address: result[0], lease: result[1] };<br>} catch(e) {<br>  console.error(e);<br>  return undefined;<br>}</pre>
           <p name="d170" id="d170" class="graf graf--p graf-after--pre">Here we wait until a particular element has rendered on the page, then run a function on that page. This is where is gets weird??the JavaScript code in the&nbsp;<strong class="markup--strong markup--p-strong">.evaluate()</strong> callback is run <em class="markup--em markup--p-em">in the execution context of the browser</em>. That means its run in the same environment that the page is running JavaScript inthe code running in the browser has no knowledge of your Nightmare code otherwise. You can even inject entire JS/CSS files into the page if you so desire. What we do is use <strong class="markup--strong markup--p-strong">document.querySelectorAll</strong> to retrieve all of the oddly-named&nbsp;<strong class="markup--strong markup--p-strong">.w80p </strong>elements, of which there are two: the address and the lease type, both of which are useful pieces of data. Because <strong class="markup--strong markup--p-strong">document.querySelectorAll </strong>returns a NodeList, which doesnt have <strong class="markup--strong markup--p-strong">Array.prototype.map</strong> available to it, I use the ES6 spread operator inside of an array to convert it to one. I then use&nbsp;<strong class="markup--strong markup--p-strong">.map() </strong>to return an array containing the inner text of each element. This array is what is assigned to <em class="markup--em markup--p-em">result</em> when the sequence resolves. If you supply a value after the callback function in&nbsp;<strong class="markup--strong markup--p-strong">.evaluate()</strong>, you can pass in arbitrary variables like this:</p>
           <pre name="c2b0" id="c2b0" class="graf graf--pre graf-after--p">.evaluate(outer =&gt; { <br>  console.log(`This is from the outer scope: ${outer}`);<br>}, variableFromOuterScope);</pre>
           <p name="af77" id="af77" class="graf graf--p graf-after--pre">Again, the Electron instance Nightmare is running isnt really aware of that fact, so any variables defined elsewhere in your scope wont otherwise be available to&nbsp;<strong class="markup--strong markup--p-strong">.evaluate()</strong>, which is admittedly sort of antithetical to how scopes normally work in JavaScriptjust realize the variable scoping provided by the&nbsp;<strong class="markup--strong markup--p-strong">.evaluate() </strong>callback is entirely separate to that from the rest of the script.</p>
           <p name="f50a" id="f50a" class="graf graf--p graf-after--p">After our&nbsp;<strong class="markup--strong markup--p-strong">.evaluate()</strong> call, we call&nbsp;<strong class="markup--strong markup--p-strong">.end()</strong> to close the browser window and free up memory. Assuming everything worked well, the results from&nbsp;<strong class="markup--strong markup--p-strong">.evaluate()</strong> will be assigned to <strong class="markup--strong markup--p-strong"><em class="markup--em markup--p-em">result</em></strong> and we can then return that as part of an object.</p>
           <p name="ecaa" id="ecaa" class="graf graf--p graf-after--p">Lets try it with just one land registry number. Put the following after our function:</p>
           <pre name="fabe" id="fabe" class="graf graf--pre graf-after--p">getAddress(numbers[0])<br>  .then(a =&gt; console.dir(a))<br>  .catch(e =&gt; console.error(e));</pre>
           <p name="a979" id="a979" class="graf graf--p graf-after--pre">Remember, <strong class="markup--strong markup--p-strong">getAddress()</strong> is an <em class="markup--em markup--p-em">async</em> function, which means it returns a promise. Promises all have a&nbsp;<strong class="markup--strong markup--p-strong">.then() </strong>method that is called once the promise has been resolved, and a&nbsp;<strong class="markup--strong markup--p-strong">.catch()</strong> method for handling errors. Because we can only use <strong class="markup--strong markup--p-strong"><em class="markup--em markup--p-em">await </em></strong>inside of an asynchronous function (which the main process is not), we use normal promise handling here, just echoing it out into our console. Again,&nbsp;.<strong class="markup--strong markup--p-strong">then()</strong> is the non-<strong class="markup--strong markup--p-strong"><em class="markup--em markup--p-em">async</em></strong> function version of <strong class="markup--strong markup--p-strong"><em class="markup--em markup--p-em">await,</em> </strong>and&nbsp;<strong class="markup--strong markup--p-strong">.catch()</strong> is what you use instead of <strong class="markup--strong markup--p-strong"><em class="markup--em markup--p-em">try/catch</em></strong> outside of an <strong class="markup--strong markup--p-strong"><em class="markup--em markup--p-em">async</em></strong> function to handle problems. They ultimately work a bit different but that explanation will suffice for now.</p>
           <p name="bc9b" id="bc9b" class="graf graf--p graf-after--p">Time to try running our scraper:</p>
           <pre name="cdf8" id="cdf8" class="graf graf--pre graf-after--p">$ node --harmony index.js</pre>
           <p name="e244" id="e244" class="graf graf--p graf-after--pre">Cool! That seems like usable data!</p>
           <p name="4fe1" id="4fe1" class="graf graf--p graf-after--p">Time to do things as a sequential loop. Here we encounter another slight difficulty with promises, which is the fact that they arent blocking. This is actually a really good thing, but in this particular instance we dont want to fry the Land Registry servers by hitting it with all of our requests simultaneously. Luckily, we can also use promises to manage this for us:</p>
           <pre name="b3a0" id="b3a0" class="graf graf--pre graf-after--p">const series = numbers.reduce(async (queue, number) =&gt; {<br>  const dataArray = await queue;<br>  dataArray.push(await getAddress(number));<br>  return dataArray;<br>}, Promise.resolve([]));</pre>
           <p name="59ea" id="59ea" class="graf graf--p graf-after--pre">This is actually really cool, let me explain it:</p>
           <p name="64a0" id="64a0" class="graf graf--p graf-after--p"><strong class="markup--strong markup--p-strong"><em class="markup--em markup--p-em">numbers</em></strong> is an array of values. Because of that, we can call <a href="https://developer.mozilla.org/en/docs/Web/JavaScript/Reference/Global_Objects/Array/reduce" data-href="https://developer.mozilla.org/en/docs/Web/JavaScript/Reference/Global_Objects/Array/reduce" class="markup--anchor markup--p-anchor" rel="noopener" target="_blank"><strong class="markup--strong markup--p-strong">Array.prototype.reduce()</strong></a> on it. What happens here is we create a promise chain that initially resolves to an empty array, that we gradually add to as each of our <strong class="markup--strong markup--p-strong">getAddress()</strong> operations completes. Effectively, what were doing is adding a new operation to our queue of promises for each element in the array, then resolving them.</p>
           <p name="0fc8" id="0fc8" class="graf graf--p graf-after--p">If you try to log the resulting <strong class="markup--strong markup--p-strong"><em class="markup--em markup--p-em">series</em></strong> variable to console, youll just get a boatload of pending promises. Thats because <strong class="markup--strong markup--p-strong">console.log()</strong> runs immediately following the&nbsp;<strong class="markup--strong markup--p-strong">.reduce()</strong> operation and none of the promises have resolved yet. Instead, what we need to do is call&nbsp;<strong class="markup--strong markup--p-strong">.then()</strong> on our <strong class="markup--strong markup--p-strong"><em class="markup--em markup--p-em">series </em></strong>variable so we get our array of resolved promises once our queue finishes.</p>
           <pre name="825f" id="825f" class="graf graf--pre graf-after--p">series.then(data =&gt; {<br>  const csvData = csvFormat(data.filter(i =&gt; i));<br>  writeFileSync('./output.csv', csvData, { encoding: 'utf8' });<br>})<br>.catch(e =&gt; console.error(e));</pre>
           <p name="0b79" id="0b79" class="graf graf--p graf-after--pre">Here we first <strong class="markup--strong markup--p-strong">filter</strong> out any <strong class="markup--strong markup--p-strong"><em class="markup--em markup--p-em">undefined</em></strong> array elements (caused by a failed iteration of our scraper) using an identity function, and then use <strong class="markup--strong markup--p-strong">csvFormat()</strong> to reformat our array as a CSV string so we can write it out, which we do with <strong class="markup--strong markup--p-strong">writeFileSync()</strong>. Run our scraper again with:</p>
           <pre name="a7b6" id="a7b6" class="graf graf--pre graf-after--p">$ node --harmony index.js</pre>
           <p name="8125" id="8125" class="graf graf--p graf-after--pre">And youll see Nightmare slice through that list of values like its hot butter!</p>
           <p name="f538" id="f538" class="graf graf--p graf-after--p">Youll notice at least one of those values doesnt actually work??yet, because weve gracefully handled exceptions, Nightmare waits for 30 seconds and then continues on to the next request without breaking a sweat. We could, however, get it to do something else like try the request at a different URL if we needed to improve the accuracy of our scraper.</p>
           <p name="cbe3" id="cbe3" class="graf graf--p graf-after--p">After a few minutes of working through the list, the script will end and youll have a shiny new&nbsp;<strong class="markup--strong markup--p-strong">.csv</strong> file containing the addresses corresponding to each property title reference number! Tada! ?</p>
           <p name="2f71" id="2f71" class="graf graf--p graf-after--p">Whats the next step? That depends entirely on what sort of investigation youre doing. One option that might be fun given our new dataset is to geolocate it using Google Fusion Tables. Heres what that looks like:</p>
           <figure name="b2f3" id="b2f3" class="graf graf--figure graf-after--p">
            <div class="aspectRatioPlaceholder is-locked" style="max-width: 700px; max-height: 647px;">
             <div class="aspectRatioPlaceholder-fill" style="padding-bottom: 92.4%;"></div>
             <div class="progressiveMedia js-progressiveMedia graf-image" data-image-id="1*mjzyDPqCRX0ghPt8vTY9tg.png" data-width="842" data-height="778" data-action="zoom" data-action-value="1*mjzyDPqCRX0ghPt8vTY9tg.png">
              <img src="https://cdn-images-1.medium.com/freeze/max/60/1*mjzyDPqCRX0ghPt8vTY9tg.png?q=20" crossorigin="anonymous" class="progressiveMedia-thumbnail js-progressiveMedia-thumbnail">
              <canvas class="progressiveMedia-canvas js-progressiveMedia-canvas"></canvas>
              <img class="progressiveMedia-image js-progressiveMedia-image" data-src="https://cdn-images-1.medium.com/max/1600/1*mjzyDPqCRX0ghPt8vTY9tg.png">
              <noscript class="js-progressiveMedia-inner">
               <img class="progressiveMedia-noscript js-progressiveMedia-inner" src="https://cdn-images-1.medium.com/max/1600/1*mjzyDPqCRX0ghPt8vTY9tg.png">
              </noscript>
             </div>
            </div>
            <figcaption class="imageCaption">
             Heres the locations mapped in Google Fusion Tables. All I had to do was set the 
             <strong class="markup--strong markup--figure-strong"><em class="markup--em markup--figure-em">address</em></strong> column as a location, which I then geocoded using File&nbsp;» Geocode. You can view the finished Fusion Table&nbsp;
             <a href="https://www.google.com/fusiontables/DataSource?docid=1FEtG--p7NCM8T9reIojtcE16bXuI5w24ezY6_mUi" data-href="https://www.google.com/fusiontables/DataSource?docid=1FEtG--p7NCM8T9reIojtcE16bXuI5w24ezY6_mUi" class="markup--anchor markup--figure-anchor" rel="noopener" target="_blank">here</a>.
            </figcaption>
           </figure>
           <p name="9856" id="9856" class="graf graf--p graf-after--figure">Excepting the one point that somehow geolocated in India, seems like most of the locations we mapped are near Southampton.</p>
           <p name="06de" id="06de" class="graf graf--p graf-after--p">Thats all there is to it. Nightmare can do a fair bit morefor instance, it can listen to events and react accordingly, making it possible to even scrape things like websocket-based chat systems, and can even take screenshots while it works (Or possibly as part of exception handling, allowing you to see exactly where a scraper task failed). <a href="https://github.com/segmentio/nightmare" data-href="https://github.com/segmentio/nightmare" class="markup--anchor markup--p-anchor" rel="noopener" target="_blank">Read the documentation</a> for full details.</p>
           <p name="45dd" id="45dd" class="graf graf--p graf-after--p"><strong class="markup--strong markup--p-strong">Other notes:</strong></p>
           <ul class="postList">
            <li name="c088" id="c088" class="graf graf--li graf-after--p">Nightmares&nbsp;<strong class="markup--strong markup--li-strong">.wait() </strong>method is really key to doing things intelligently, particularly after clicking a link or navigating in some way. It can take a CSS selector, a number in milliseconds or a function as an argument (that last option working like&nbsp;<strong class="markup--strong markup--li-strong">.evaluate()</strong> as per the explanation earlier).</li>
            <li name="d973" id="d973" class="graf graf--li graf-after--li">In this example, we wait until everything is done and then pass that to a single function to do all the writing. In reality, this is incredibly prone to error as it means the entire queue must complete before anything is saved. In reality, you probably want to add some logic that writes to disk on every completed operation, skipping the completed tasks if you need to restart the scraper.</li>
            <li name="9293" id="9293" class="graf graf--li graf-after--li">Set <strong class="markup--strong markup--li-strong"><em class="markup--em markup--li-em">show</em></strong> to <strong class="markup--strong markup--li-strong"><em class="markup--em markup--li-em">false</em></strong> to hide the Electron window. It can be kind of annoying keeping it open as it steals cursor focus and prevents you from doing anything else while the scraper runs, but having it open can be helpful when first writing your scraper as it lets you monitor what Nightmare is doing.</li>
            <li name="4aea" id="4aea" class="graf graf--li graf-after--li">You can use <a href="http://bluebirdjs.com/docs/getting-started.html" data-href="http://bluebirdjs.com/docs/getting-started.html" class="markup--anchor markup--li-anchor" rel="noopener" target="_blank">Bluebird</a> or some other promise implementation if you prefer, but Ive generally not found it to be more worthwhile than the native ES6 implementation.</li>
            <li name="4d9a" id="4d9a" class="graf graf--li graf-after--li">You can also use ES7 generators with Nightmare, but thats way less fun than using async/await as detailed above.</li>
            <li name="9d6e" id="9d6e" class="graf graf--li graf-after--li">Always consider the potential damage a scraper might do and balance that against the public interest. If you hit a website with 5000 simultaneous requests, it might appear to be a Denial-Of-Service (DoS) attack and you may have some splainin to do.</li>
           </ul>
           <blockquote name="ea1d" id="ea1d" class="graf graf--blockquote graf-after--li">
            <a href="https://medium.com/@aendrew" data-href="https://medium.com/@aendrew" data-anchor-type="2" data-user-id="63d185583afa" data-action-value="63d185583afa" data-action="show-user-card" data-action-type="hover" class="markup--user markup--blockquote-user" target="_blank">Ændrew Rininsland</a> is the author of 
            <a href="https://www.packtpub.com/web-development/data-visualization-d3js" data-href="https://www.packtpub.com/web-development/data-visualization-d3js" class="markup--anchor markup--blockquote-anchor" rel="nofollow noopener" target="_blank"><strong class="markup--strong markup--blockquote-strong">Data Visualization with D3.js, 2nd edition</strong> from Packt Books</a> and a newsroom developer at the 
            <a href="https://www.ft.com/" data-href="https://www.ft.com/" class="markup--anchor markup--blockquote-anchor" rel="nofollow noopener" target="_blank">Financial Times</a>.&nbsp;
            <br>He tweets as 
            <a href="https://www.twitter.com/aendrew/" data-href="https://www.twitter.com/aendrew/" class="markup--anchor markup--blockquote-anchor" rel="nofollow noopener" target="_blank">@aendrew</a>.
